▼Is Epicurean Philosophy Straightforward About What It Advocates, or Does It Advocate Something Not Visible On The Surface?
Commentary
Epicurus deliberately built a philosophy that ordinary people could grasp without technical education: the goal (pleasure), the method (trust the senses, anticipations, and feelings), and the practical guidance (examine your desires; secure the natural and necessary ones first) require no specialized training to understand or to act on. Cicero's own testimony below is the most persuasive evidence for this point precisely because it is hostile: he complains that Epicureans recruit uneducated villagers who somehow grasp Epicurus's meaning while he, a trained orator, professes confusion. The plainness of the philosophy was never a defect in Epicurus's eyes -- it was the point. A system of ethics that requires decades of technical schooling before a person can act on it is useless to the great majority of human beings, who need to live well now.
Cassius Longinus to Cicero, January, 45 BCEpicurusEasyToUnderstandVirtueAsContextual
Cassius to Cicero Brundisium, latter half of January, 45 B.C. I hope that you are well. I assure you that on this tour of mine there is nothing that gives me more pleasure to do than to write to you; for I seem to be talking and joking with you face to face. And yet that does not come to pass because of those “spectres”; and, by way of retaliation for that, in my next letter I shall let loose upon you such a rabble of Stoic boors that you will proclaim Catius a true-born Athenian. I am glad that our friend Pansa was sped on his way by universal goodwill when he left the city in military uniform, and that not only on my own account, but also, most assuredly, on that of all our friends. For I hope that men generally will come to understand how much all the world hates cruelty, and how much it loves integrity and clemency, and that the blessings most eagerly sought and coveted by the bad ultimately find their way to the good. For it is hard to convince men that “the good is to be chosen for its own sake”; but that pleasure and tranquillity of mind is acquired by virtue, justice, and the good is both true and demonstrable. Why, Epicurus himself, from whom all the Catiuses and Amafiniuses in the world, incompetent translators of terms as they are, derive their origin, lays it down that “to live a life of pleasure is impossible without living a life of virtue and justice.”
Cicero, On Ends II.viii (Reid translation)EpicurusEasyToUnderstand
[Cicero, arguing against the Epicurean Torquatus, complains that Epicurus's followers are recruited from the uneducated -- and concedes, in doing so, that they understand Epicurus perfectly well.] So you are in the habit of telling us this, very often, that we do not understand what kind of pleasure Epicurus means. Now whenever I have been told this... I have the habit of getting now and then a little angry, though I usually bear myself with tolerable calmness in discussion. Do I not understand what ἡδονὴ means in Greek and voluptas in Latin? Which, pray, of the two languages is it that I do not know? Next, how comes it that I do not know this, though all those are aware of it, whoever they be, that have chosen to become Epicureans? And this is a point argued by your school most admirably, that a man who is to become a philosopher has no need to be acquainted with literature. Thus just as our ancestors brought old Cincinnatus from his plough to make him dictator, so you gather from every village men who are indeed worthies, but surely not very well educated. They then understand what Epicurus means, and I do not?
Cicero, On Ends II.v (Yonge translation)EpicurusEasyToUnderstand
[Cicero admits that Epicurus is not writing about some obscure, technical subject -- but about pleasure, a plain matter of everyday common conversation.] Epicurus, as I imagine, is both willing, if it is in his power, to speak intelligibly, and is also speaking, not of an obscure subject like the natural philosophers, nor of one depending on precise rules, as the mathematicians are, but he is discussing a plain and simple matter, which is a subject of common conversation among the common people.
Cicero, Tusculan Disputations I.iii-iv (Yonge translation)EpicurusEasyToUnderstand
[Cicero, recounting how Epicurean philosophy spread rapidly through Italy after Amafinius first published on the subject in Latin, explicitly attributes its success to how easily its doctrines are understood by the unlearned.] During this silence, C. Amafinius arose and took upon himself to speak; on the publishing of whose writings the people were moved, and enlisted themselves chiefly under this sect, either because the doctrine was more easily understood, or because they were invited thereto by the pleasing thoughts of amusement, or that, because there was nothing better, they laid hold of what was offered them. And after Amafinius, when many of the same sentiments had written much about them, [these doctrines] spread over all Italy: but that these doctrines should be so easily understood and approved of by the unlearned, is a great proof that they were not written with any great subtlety.
Cicero, Tusculan Disputations III:XXI (Yonge translation)EpicurusEasyToUnderstand
[Cicero, in the midst of arguing against Epicurus, grudgingly concedes the good character of Epicurus's followers -- inadvertent testimony from a hostile witness.] And indeed the Epicureans, those best of men, for there is no order of men more innocent, complain that I take great pains to inveigh against Epicurus. We are rivals, I suppose, for some honour or distinction. I place the chief good in the mind, he in the body; I in virtue, he in pleasure; and the Epicureans are up in arms, and implore the assistance of their neighbours, and many are ready to fly to their aid.
▼Does Epicurean Physics Remain Valuable, Or Has It Been Made Obsolete By Modern Science?EpicureanPhysicsNotObsolete
Commentary
Epicurean physics is often dismissed as a historical curiosity superseded by modern science, but this confuses two different levels of claim. The specific mechanisms Epicurus proposed for particular phenomena -- the size of the sun, the causes of earthquakes -- were always offered provisionally, and Epicurus said so openly. But the foundational propositions -- that nothing comes from nothing, that the universe is composed of matter and void, that matter is conserved -- are logical arguments from universal experience, not empirical guesses awaiting better instruments. Conservation of matter and energy, atomic theory, and a universe with no true beginning or end (in some form) remain core commitments of modern physics as well. The framework endures because it was never a science textbook to begin with -- it was philosophy's guardrail against superstition, and that job is not finished.
Epicurus, Principal Doctrine 12EpicureanPhysicsNotObsoleteScienceNecessaryForHappiness
[Epicurus states the purpose of physics directly: it is not a catalogue of provisional facts to be overturned by later instruments, but the necessary logical framework -- atoms and void, the eternity of matter -- that removes superstitious fear. That foundational framework is what modern science has in key respects confirmed rather than refuted.] A man cannot dispel his fear about the most important matters if he does not know what is the nature of the universe but suspects the truth of some mythical story. So that without natural science it is not possible to attain our pleasures unalloyed.
▼Is It True That Epicurus Was Not An Atheist As We Today Understand That Term?EpicurusNotAnAtheist
Commentary
The charge of atheism against Epicurus is understandable but wrong, and Epicurus's own words settle it directly: "gods there are, since the knowledge of them is by clear vision." What Epicurus denied was not the existence of divine beings but the popular mythology attached to them -- the belief that gods are supernatural and punish, reward, or otherwise intervene in human affairs out of anger or favor. He defined piety in reverse: the truly impious person is not the one who denies the gods of popular superstition, but the one who projects human pettiness -- anger, favor, meddling -- onto beings whose blessed and immortal nature, by definition, cannot include those things. This is a correction of theology, not a denial of divinity.
Epicurus, Letter to Menoeceus [123]EpicurusNotAnAtheist
[Epicurus states plainly that gods exist. His quarrel is not with their existence but with the popular superstitious beliefs attached to them -- that they punish, reward, or otherwise meddle in human affairs.]
Cicero, On the Nature of the Gods 1.44 (Rackham translation)EpicurusNotAnAtheistNatureOfGods
[This is the ancient charge -- via the Stoic Posidonius, as reported by the hostile Cicero -- that Epicurus was a covert atheist who retained the gods in name only to avoid popular odium. Epicurus's own texts elsewhere refute this by affirming the gods' real existence while correcting mistaken beliefs about their nature.] It is doubtless therefore truer to say, as the good friend of us all, Posidonius, argued in the fifth book of his On the Nature of the Gods, that Epicurus does not really believe in the gods at all, and that he said what he did about the immortal gods only for the sake of deprecating popular odium. In the first place, a being of this nature is an absolute impossibility, and Epicurus was aware of this, and so actually abolishes the gods, although professedly retaining them. First of all believe that god is a being immortal and blessed, even as the common idea of a god is engraved on men's minds, and do not assign to him anything alien to his immortality or ill-suited to his blessedness: but believe about him everything that can uphold his blessedness and immortality. For gods there are, since the knowledge of them is by clear vision. But they are not such as the many believe them to be: for indeed they do not consistently represent them as they believe them to be. And the impious man is not he who popularly denies the gods of the many, but he who attaches to the gods the beliefs of the many.
▼Are Anticipations True or False, Or Are They Neither As They Are Not Opinions?AnticipationsAreNotOpinions
Commentary
Anticipations (prolepseis) occupy a distinct category in the Epicurean Canon, separate from and prior to opinion. A preconception -- the general concept of "man" or "horse" built up from repeated experience -- is not itself a claim that can be true or false; it is the standard against which claims (opinions) are subsequently checked. When someone asks whether the thing standing yonder is a horse or a cow, they can only investigate the question because they already possess, prior to any judgment, the preconception of what a horse and a cow look like. Opinion is the judgment added on top of a preconception or a sensation, and it is opinion -- not the anticipation itself -- that can go wrong. Treating anticipations as if they were themselves opinions collapses a distinction Epicurus considered foundational to the whole theory of knowledge.
Diogenes Laertius X.33 (Letter to Herodotus)AnticipationsAreNotOpinionsCriteriaOfTruth
[Epicurus establishes preconceptions (prolepsis) as a foundational criterion of truth in their own right, logically prior to and distinct from opinion (doxa) -- an opinion is judged true or false by reference back to the preconception, not the other way around.] The concept they speak of as an apprehension or right opinion or thought or general idea stored within the mind, that is to say a recollection of what has often been presented from without... Therefore the first signification of every name is immediate and clear evidence. And we could not look for the object of our search, unless we have first known it... So the concepts are clear and immediate evidence. Further, the decision of opinion depends on some previous clear and immediate evidence, to which we refer when we express it.
▼Was Epicurus A Complete Empiricist, Or Did He Rely on Reason In Reaching His Conclusions?EpicurusNotCompleteEmpiricist
Commentary
Epicurus grounded all knowledge in the senses, but he was not a naive empiricist who accepted only what could be directly observed. His Canon explicitly authorizes reasoned inference about things imperceptible to the senses -- atoms, void, the causes of distant phenomena -- built from analogy, combination, and similarity with what is actually observed. This inferential method, later developed at length by his followers in the debate over sign-inference, is how Epicurus justified confidence in unseen entities like atoms while keeping that reasoning tethered to sensory evidence at every step. The difference between Epicurus and a naive empiricist is that his reasoning about the non-evident was always disciplined by and answerable to direct experience -- never permitted to float free of it, as Platonic or Stoic abstraction did.
Diogenes Laertius X.32 (Letter to Herodotus)EpicurusNotCompleteEmpiricistSensationAsBasisOfKnowledgeMethodOfReasoning
[Epicurus grounds knowledge of the non-evident -- atoms, void, and other things imperceptible to the senses -- in reasoned inference from what is perceived, not in direct sensation alone. This is why Epicurus cannot fairly be called a naive or complete empiricist who accepted only raw sense-data.] Nor is there anything which can refute the sensations... From this it follows that as regards the imperceptible we must draw inferences from phenomena. For all thoughts have their origin in sensations by means of coincidence and analogy and similarity and combination, reasoning too contributing something.
▼Not All Desire Is Itself PainfulAllDesireNotPainful
Commentary
It is sometimes claimed that desire itself is inherently a form of suffering, so that the only escape from pain is the extinguishing of desire altogether. Epicurus's own classification of desires refutes this directly: only some desires cause pain if left unfulfilled -- the natural and necessary ones; others, left unfulfilled, involve nothing worse than a craving that is easily dispelled, with no pain attached at all. Desire is not uniformly painful; whether an unfulfilled desire hurts depends on which category it falls into and how firmly it is examined. This is why Epicurus's answer to an unwanted desire was never simply "extinguish all desire," but "examine the desire and ask what will happen if it is, or is not, satisfied."
Epicurus, Principal Doctrine 26AllDesireNotPainfulCalculatingDesires
[Epicurus distinguishes desires whose non-fulfillment causes pain (the necessary desires) from those whose non-fulfillment causes no pain at all, only an easily-dispelled craving -- directly against the claim that all unsatisfied desire is itself a species of suffering.] Of desires, all that do not lead to a sense of pain, if they are not satisfied, are not necessary, but involve a craving which is easily dispelled, when the object is hard to procure or they seem likely to produce harm.
▼Does "Live Unknown" Mean To Live In Isolation? If Not, What Does It Mean?LiveUnknownDoesNotMeanIsolation
Commentary
"Live unknown" (lathe biosas) is routinely quoted, especially by hostile ancient sources like Plutarch, as if it counseled total withdrawal from the world. But the advice was aimed at the pursuit of political careerism, reputation, and office specifically -- not at social or civic invisibility as such. The historical record contradicts the isolationist reading directly: the Garden sat on Athens's busiest ceremonial road, its gate carried a welcoming sign addressed to strangers, and Epicurus corresponded prolifically with students and friends across the Greek world. Declining to compete for political office and public fame is not the same thing as declining to engage with other human beings.
Epicurus, Vatican Saying 86 and Vatican Saying 58LiveUnknownDoesNotMeanIsolation
[The advice to "live unknown" (lathe biosas), reported by Plutarch, is aimed at the pursuit of political reputation and office, not at civic invisibility as such -- the Garden stood on Athens's most traveled road with a sign addressed to strangers, and Epicurus maintained an enormous correspondence. See the companion article "Not A Bunker But A Camp" for the fuller argument.] Live unknown. We must release ourselves from the prison of affairs and politics.
▼Does Avoiding Politics Mean Passive Withdrawal From The World?NoPoliticsDoesNotMeanQuietist
Commentary
Epicurus's advice to avoid formal office-seeking is often mistaken for a blanket counsel of passivity. But Diogenes Laertius's own description of the Epicurean Wise Man shows an active life: he engages in lawsuits, writes and leaves behind treatises, gathers a school, and lectures in public when asked. What he avoids is the specific mechanism of formal political ambition -- competing for office, delivering set-piece political oratory -- because that mechanism ties one's happiness to the unstable opinions of a crowd. The historical example of Cassius Longinus, an open and self-identified Epicurean who organized armed resistance to Caesar's tyranny, along with other Epicurean figures in Roman public life, puts the passive-withdrawal caricature to rest.
Diogenes Laertius X.119-121NoPoliticsDoesNotMeanQuietistHowToLive
[The Wise Man avoids conventional office-seeking and set-piece political oratory -- but this same passage describes an active life: lawsuits, writing, teaching, gathering a school, public lectures. Declining electoral politics is not passive withdrawal from engagement with the world.] Nor will he take part in public life, as he says in the first book On Lives. Nor will he act the tyrant, or live like the Cynics... He will engage in lawsuits and will leave writings behind him, but will not deliver speeches on public occasions. He will be careful of his possessions and will provide for the future... He will care more than other men for public spectacles. And he will gather together a school, but never so as to become a popular leader. He will give lectures in public, but never unless asked; he will give definite teaching and not profess doubt. He will pay court to a king, if occasion demands. (Bailey)
Epicurus, Principal Doctrine 14NoPoliticsDoesNotMeanQuietist
The most unalloyed source of protection from men, which is secured to some extent by a certain force of expulsion, is in fact the immunity which results from a quiet life and the retirement from the world. (Bailey)
▼Is Katastematic Pleasure The Only Pleasure Or The Best Pleasure, Or is It Only One Type Of Pleasure?KatastematicNotBestPleasure
Commentary
Later commentary, especially hostile Stoic and Academic sources, sometimes claims that Epicurus recognized only static (katastematic) pleasure -- the mere absence of disturbance -- as legitimate, treating kinetic pleasures of motion (joy, delight, active enjoyment) as secondary. Diogenes Laertius reports Epicurus's own position directly: he admitted both kinds of pleasure, in both the body and the soul, and named each -- freedom from trouble and pain are static pleasures, while joy and exultation are kinetic ones. Neither kind is elevated as the only pleasure that counts; both are integrated into the full range of pleasurable experience that the complete Epicurean life includes. The static/kinetic distinction describes two different kinds of pleasure -- it is not a hierarchy between a superior and an inferior kind.
Diogenes Laertius X.136KatastematicNotBestPleasure
[Epicurus explicitly affirms both static (katastematic) and kinetic pleasures on equal footing -- against any reading that treats static pleasure as the only pleasure or the exclusively best pleasure, with kinetic pleasures merely tolerated or lesser.] Epicurus differs from the Cyrenaics about pleasure. For they do not admit static pleasure, but only that which consists in motion. But Epicurus admits both kinds both in the soul and in the body... Freedom from trouble in the mind and from pain in the body are static pleasures, but Joy and exultation are considered as active pleasures involving motion.
Diogenes of Oinoanda, Fragment 34KatastematicNotBestPleasure
[The quantitative limit of pleasure is the removal of all pain -- and once that static limit is reached, whoever experiences it cannot be troubled by pain of body or of mind. This is Diogenes's own restatement of the static/kinetic point: static pleasure is the limit pleasure reaches, not the only kind of pleasure that counts.] When the emotions which disturb the soul are removed, those which produce pleasure enter into it to take their place.
▼Does Happiness Require The Complete Absence Of All Pain, And If So How Could Epicurus Say He Was Happy While Dying Of Kidney Disease?HappinessDoesNotRequireCompleteAbsenceOfPain
Commentary
If happiness required the total absence of all pain, Epicurus's own deathbed testimony would be incoherent -- he was dying in severe pain from disease of the bladder and stomach, and he called it "this truly happy day of my life." The resolution is that happiness is a property of a whole, weighed life, not a fragile state destroyed by any pain whatsoever - even a severe one. Principal Doctrine 4 states plainly that chronic illness can still leave a predominance of pleasure over pain in the flesh. The limit of pleasure is reached when all pain is removed, but reaching that limit in the mind, through right understanding of what matters, does not require that the body and mind never experience pain at all.
Epicurus, Letter to Idomeneus (his last letter, on his deathbed)HappinessDoesNotRequireCompleteAbsenceOfPainFriendshipAsPleasurable
[Epicurus, dying in severe physical pain, calls this his "truly happy day" -- direct testimony that happiness is compatible with the ongoing presence of bodily pain, so long as it is outweighed by the pleasures of the mind.] On this truly happy day of my life, as I am at the point of death, I write this to you. The disease in my bladder and stomach are pursuing their course, lacking nothing of their natural severity: but against all this is the joy in my heart at the recollection of my conversations with you.
Epicurus, Principal Doctrine 4HappinessDoesNotRequireCompleteAbsenceOfPain
Pain does not last continuously in the flesh, but the acutest pain is there for a very short time, and even that which just exceeds the pleasure in the flesh does not continue for many days at once. But chronic illnesses permit a predominance of pleasure over pain in the flesh.
Diogenes of Oinoanda, Fragment 44 (Lower Margin)HappinessDoesNotRequireCompleteAbsenceOfPain
[Diogenes restates Principal Doctrine 4 in his own words: bodily pain does not last, and even chronic conditions leave a predominance of pleasure -- direct corroborating testimony for why Epicurus's deathbed happiness is not a contradiction.] Pain in the flesh does not last continuously: extreme pain is present a very short time; pain which only just outweighs pleasure in the flesh does not last many days; and chronic illnesses permit a preponderance of pleasure over pain in the flesh. (Smith)
▼Does An Epicurean Limit Himself Only To Desires That Are Natural And Necessary, Or Can He Pursue "Unnecessary" Desires That Lead To More Pleasure Than Pain?NotLimitedToNaturalAndNecessaryDesires
Commentary
A persistent misreading reduces Epicurus to a philosopher of minimalism, as if only the bare natural-and-necessary desires -- food, shelter, basic security -- are legitimate to pursue. Epicurus's own words say otherwise: Vatican Saying 21 explicitly endorses fulfilling "the physical" desires -- natural but not strictly necessary -- so long as they cause no harm, which is best understood as more pain than pleasure. The Letter to Menoeceus praises the ability to enjoy luxury when it is available without becoming dependent on it. The three-category classification of desires (natural-and-necessary, natural-but-unnecessary, neither natural nor necessary) is a tool for judging which desires are worth pursuing and at what cost -- not a fence restricting the good life to only the first category.
Epicurus, Vatican Saying 21NotLimitedToNaturalAndNecessaryDesiresCalculatingDesires
[Epicurus endorses fulfilling natural desires beyond the strictly necessary ones -- the "physical" (natural-but-unnecessary) desires -- so long as they bring no harm. The good life is not restricted to the bare minimum of natural and necessary desire alone.] We must not violate nature, but obey her; and we shall obey her if we fulfill the necessary desires and also the physical, if they bring no harm to us, but sternly reject the harmful.
Epicurus, Letter to Menoeceus [130]NotLimitedToNaturalAndNecessaryDesiresCalculatingDesires
Independence of desire we think a great good -- not that we may at all times enjoy but a few things, but that, if we do not possess many, we may enjoy the few in the genuine persuasion that those have the sweetest pleasure in luxury who least need it... And so plain savours bring us a pleasure equal to a luxurious diet, when all the pain due to want is removed.
▼Are Sex, Marriage, And Children Things To Be Pursued, Or Not, And How Do You Know?SexMarriageAndChildrenAreNatural
Commentary
Sexual desire, romantic partnership, and the desire for children belong among the natural desires in Epicurus's own classification -- as natural as hunger and thirst -- and Diogenes Laertius reports directly that Epicurus taught the wise man will marry and have children, adapting the decision to his own circumstances in life. Lucretius develops the same point at length in Book IV of On The Nature Of Things, tracing sexual desire to nature's own design while distinguishing the natural pleasure of the act from the pathology of obsessive, deranged romantic passion. Nothing in Epicurean philosophy recommends celibacy or withdrawal from ordinary family life as a superior path -- the philosophy was never designed for monks. How to know whether a particular pursuit of sex, marriage, or children serves pleasure in a given case is answered the same way any other desire is judged: by weighing the actual balance of pleasure and pain it produces in one's own circumstances, not by a rule that forbids or requires it in advance.
Diogenes Laertius X.119SexMarriageAndChildrenAreNatural
[Direct testimony that Epicurus taught the wise man will marry and have children -- a natural and normal part of life, not something to be avoided by the philosopher.] Moreover, the wise man will marry and have children, as Epicurus says in the Problems and in the work On Nature. But he will marry according to the circumstances of his life.
